</table>
<table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;width: 22em;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="263" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Hangugeo-Chosonmal.svg/28px-Hangugeo-Chosonmal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Hangugeo-Chosonmal.svg/42px-Hangugeo-Chosonmal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Hangugeo-Chosonmal.svg/57px-Hangugeo-Chosonmal.svg.png 2x" width="28"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <strong class="selflink">Korean</strong> text.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support_(East_Asian)" title="Help:Multilingual support (East Asian)">rendering support</a>, you may see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a>.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Korean language</b> (<span lang="ko"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%ED%95%9C%EA%B5%AD%EC%96%B4" title="wikt:한국어">한국어</a></span>, see <a href="#Names">below</a>) is the official <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">Republic of Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">Democratic People's Republic of Korea</a>, with different official forms used in each nation-state; it is also one of the two official languages in the <a href="/wiki/Yanbian_Korean_Autonomous_Prefecture" title="Yanbian Korean Autonomous Prefecture">Yanbian Korean Autonomous Prefecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Changbai_Korean_Autonomous_County" title="Changbai Korean Autonomous County">Changbai Korean Autonomous County</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>. Approximately 80 million people worldwide speak Korean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google10_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-google10-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Korean has a few <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">extinct</a> relatives, which together with Korean itself form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koreanic_language" title="Koreanic language">Koreanic language family</a>. Despite this, historical and modern linguists classify Korean as a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google15_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-google15-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The idea that Korean belongs to the controversial <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> language family or to the <a href="/wiki/Dravido-Korean_languages" title="Dravido-Korean languages">Dravido-Korean family</a> has been generally discredited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> There is still debate on whether Korean and Japanese are related languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The Korean language is <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative</a> in its morphology and <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">SOV</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Classification"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Classification</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Geographic_distribution_and_international_spread"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Geographic distribution and international spread</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Korean_language" title="History of the Korean language">History of the Korean language</a></div>
<p>Modern Korean descends from <a href="/wiki/History_of_Korean#Middle_Korean" title="History of Korean">Middle Korean</a>, which in turn descends from <a href="/wiki/Old_Korean" title="Old Korean">Old Korean</a>, which descends from the language spoken in <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Korea" title="Prehistoric Korea">Prehistoric Korea</a> (labeled Proto-Korean), whose nature is debated, in part because Korean genetic origins are controversial. A relation of Korean (together with its extinct relatives which form the <a href="/wiki/Koreanic_languages" title="Koreanic languages">Koreanic family</a>) with <a href="/wiki/Japonic_languages" title="Japonic languages">Japonic languages</a> has been proposed by linguists like <a href="/wiki/William_George_Aston" title="William George Aston">William George Aston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Martin_(linguist)" title="Samuel Martin (linguist)">Samuel Martin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Roy_Andrew_Miller" title="Roy Andrew Miller">Roy Andrew Miller</a> and others suggested or supported the inclusion of Koreanic and Japonic languages in the purported <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic family</a> (a macro-family that would comprise <a href="/wiki/Tungusic_languages" title="Tungusic languages">Tungusic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongolian_languages" title="Mongolian languages">Mongolian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a> families), now not accepted by most specialists.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> arrived in Korea together with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> during the pre-<a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms period</a> and remained as the sole script for writing Korean through over a millennium (Chinese script adapted for Korean is known as <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a>) along with the phonetic scripts that were later invented. Only privileged elites were educated to fluently read and write, however, and most of the population was illiterate. In the 15th century, King <a href="/wiki/Sejong_the_Great" title="Sejong the Great">Sejong the Great</a> felt that the hanja were not adequate to write Korean and this was the cause of its very restricted use, so (with a likely help of the <a href="/wiki/Hall_of_Worthies" title="Hall of Worthies">Hall of Worthies</a>) he developed an <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Featural_writing_system" title="Featural writing system">featural writing system</a> known today as <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a>, which was designed to either aid in reading hanja or replace them. Introduced in the document <a href="/wiki/Hunminjeongeum" title="Hunminjeongeum">hunminjeongeum</a>, it quickly became popular and increased literacy in Korea, but it was only after the end of World War II that it was definitely widespread and made official in both North and South Koreas. Today, the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a> are largely unused in everyday life, but in South Korea they experience revivals on artistic works and are important in historic and/or linguistic studies of Korean.</p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, through 70 years of separations <a href="/wiki/North%E2%80%93South_differences_in_the_Korean_language" title="North–South differences in the Korean language">North–South differences</a> have developed in standard Korean, including variance in pronunciation, verb inflection, and vocabulary chosen.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Korean names for the language are based on the <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Korea" title="Names of Korea">names for Korea</a> used in North and South Korea.</p>
<p>In South Korea, the Korean language is referred to by many names including <i>hanguk-eo</i> "Korean language", <i>hanguk-mal</i> , "Korean speech" and <i>uri-mal</i> , "our language". In "<i>hanguk-eo</i>" and "<i>hanguk-mal</i>", the first part of the word, "<i>hanguk</i>", refers to the Korean nation while "<i>-eo</i>" and "<i>-mal</i>" mean "language" and "speech", respectively. Korean is also referred to as <i>guk-eo</i>, literally "national language". This name is based on the same Chinese characters meaning "nation" + "language" ("國語") that are also used in Taiwan and Japan to refer to their respective national languages.</p>
<p>In North Korea and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, the language is most often called <i>Chosŏn-mal</i>, or more formally, <i>Chosŏn-ŏ</i>. The English word "Korean" is derived from <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a>, which is thought to be the first dynasty known to Western countries. Korean people in the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">former USSR</a> refer to themselves as <i><a href="/wiki/Koryo-saram" title="Koryo-saram">Koryo-saram</a></i> and <i>Goryeo In</i> (literally, "<a href="/wiki/Names_of_Korea#Goryeo" title="Names of Korea">Goryeo</a> person(s)"]), and call the language <i><a href="/wiki/Koryo-mar" title="Koryo-mar">Koryo-mar</a></i>.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, following the establishment of diplomatic relations with South Korea in 1992, the term <i>Cháoxiǎnyǔ</i> or the short form: <i>Cháoyǔ</i> has normally been used to refer to the standard language of North Korea and <a href="/wiki/Yanbian_Korean_Autonomous_Prefecture" title="Yanbian Korean Autonomous Prefecture">Yanbian</a>, whereas <i>Hánguóyǔ</i> or the short form: <i>Hányǔ</i> is used to refer to the standard language of South Korea.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Some older English sources also use the spelling "Corea" to refer to the country, and its inflected form for the language and people, "Korea" becoming more popular in the late 1800s according to Google's NGram English corpus of 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoogleNGrams_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoogleNGrams-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The majority of historical and modern linguists classify Korean as a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>.</p>
<p>There are still a small number who think that Korean might be related to the now discredited Altaic family,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> but linguists agree today that typological resemblances cannot be used to prove genetic relatedness of languages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> as these features are typologically connected and <a href="/wiki/Areal_feature" title="Areal feature">easily borrowed</a> from one language to the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Such factors of typological divergence as Middle Mongolian's exhibition of gender agreement<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> can be used to argue that a genetic relationship with Altaic is unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hypothesis that Korean might be related to <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> has had some supporters due to some overlap in vocabulary and similar grammatical features that have been elaborated upon by such researchers as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Martin_(linguist)" title="Samuel Martin (linguist)">Samuel E. Martin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Andrew_Miller" title="Roy Andrew Miller">Roy Andrew Miller</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergei_Anatolyevich_Starostin" title="Sergei Anatolyevich Starostin">Sergei Anatolyevich Starostin</a> (1991) found about 25% of potential <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> in the Japanese–Korean 100-word <a href="/wiki/Swadesh_list" title="Swadesh list">Swadesh list</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Some linguists concerned with the issue, for example <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vovin" title="Alexander Vovin">Alexander Vovin</a>, have argued that the indicated similarities between Japanese and Korean are not due to any genetic relationship, but rather to a <i><a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">sprachbund</a></i> effect and heavy borrowing, especially from ancient Korean into Western Old Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A good example might be Middle Korean <i>sàm</i> and Japanese <i>asa</i>, meaning "hemp".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This word seems to be a cognate, but although it is well attested in Western Old Japanese and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ryukyuan_languages" title="Northern Ryukyuan languages">Northern Ryukyuan languages</a>, in Eastern Old Japanese it only occurs in compounds, and it is only present in three dialects of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ryukyuan_languages" title="Southern Ryukyuan languages">Southern Ryukyuan language group</a>. Also, the doublet <i>wo</i> meaning "hemp" is attested in Western Old Japanese and Southern Ryukyuan languages. It is thus plausible to assume a borrowed term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> (See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classification_of_Japonic_languages" title="Classification of Japonic languages">Classification of Japonic languages</a> for further details on a possible relationship.)</p>
<p>Among ancient languages, various closer relatives of Korean have been proposed, constituting a possible small <a href="/wiki/Koreanic_languages" title="Koreanic languages">Koreanic</a> language family. Some classify the <a href="/wiki/Jeju_language" title="Jeju language">language of Jeju Island</a> as a distinct modern Koreanic language.</p>
<p>Other famous theories are the <a href="/wiki/Dravido-Korean_languages" title="Dravido-Korean languages">Dravido-Korean languages</a> theory and the mostly unknown "southern-theory" which suggest a Austronesian relation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Korean and <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian languages</a> share similar vocabulary, both languages are agglutinative, follow the SOV order, nominal and adjectives follow the same syntax, particles are post positional, modifiers always precede modified words are some of the common features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution_and_international_spread">Geographic distribution and international spread</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution and international spread">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Korean_diaspora" title="Korean diaspora">Korean diaspora</a></div>
<p>Korean is spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Korean people</a> in North Korea and South Korea and by the <a href="/wiki/Korean_diaspora" title="Korean diaspora">Korean diaspora</a> in many countries including the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. Korean-speaking minorities exist in these states, but because of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">cultural assimilation</a> into host countries, not all ethnic Koreans may speak it with native fluency.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AH1sign-Daegu,Korea.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AH1sign-Daegu%2CKorea.jpg/220px-AH1sign-Daegu%2CKorea.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AH1sign-Daegu%2CKorea.jpg/330px-AH1sign-Daegu%2CKorea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AH1sign-Daegu%2CKorea.jpg/440px-AH1sign-Daegu%2CKorea.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AH1sign-Daegu,Korea.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Street signs in Korean; Daegu, South Korea.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Korean is the official language of South Korea and North Korea. It is also one of the two official languages of the Yanbian Korean Autonomous Prefecture in China.</p>
<p>In South Korea, the regulatory body for Korean is the <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_the_Korean_Language" title="National Institute of the Korean Language">National Institute of the Korean Language</a> , which was created by presidential decree on January 23, 1991. In North Korea, the regulatory body is the Language Institute of the Academy of Social Sciences (<span lang="ko">사회과학원 어학연구소; 社會科學院語學研究所</span>, <i>Sahoe Kwahagwon Ŏhak Yŏnguso</i>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="King_Sejong_Institute">King Sejong Institute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: King Sejong Institute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The King Sejong Institute is a central public institution supporting the <a class="external text" href="http://eng.ksif.or.kr/main.do" rel="nofollow">King Sejong Institute</a> which is the overseas educational institution of the Korean language and Korean cultures. It was established based on Article 9, Section 2 of the [Framework Act on the National Language] in order to do coordination management of the government's propagation project of the Korean language and Korean cultures.</p>
<ul>
<li>Increase in the demand for the Korean language education</li>
<li>Rapid increase in the Korean language education thanks to the spread of Hanryu, Increase in international marriage, expansion of Korean enterprises' making inroads into overseas markets, and enforcement of employment license system</li>
<li>Necessity of the National Brand representing the educational institution of the Korean language</li>
<li>Awareness of necessity of specialized brand cultivation which can do general support of overseas Korean language education based on the successful strategic propagation project of their own language</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="TOPIK_KOREA_Institute">TOPIK KOREA Institute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: TOPIK KOREA Institute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="external text" href="http://koreatopik.com/" rel="nofollow">TOPIK KOREA Institute</a> is a lifelong educational center affiliated with a variety of Korean universities in Seoul, South Korea, whose aim is to promote Korean language and culture, support local Korean teaching internationally, and facilitate cultural exchanges.</p>
<p>The TOPIK KOREA Institute is sometimes compared to language and culture promotion organizations such as King Sejong Institute. Unlike the organization, however, TOPIK KOREA Institutes operate within established universities and colleges around the world, providing educational materials.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Korean_dialects" title="Korean dialects">Korean dialects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koreanic_languages" title="Koreanic languages">Koreanic languages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koreandialects.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="422" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Koreandialects.png/300px-Koreandialects.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Koreandialects.png 1.5x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koreandialects.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dialects of Korean</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Korean has numerous small local <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a> (called <i>mal</i> (<span lang="ko">말</span>) [literally "speech"], <i>saturi</i> (<span lang="ko">사투리</span>), or <i>bang'eon</i> (<span lang="ko">방언</span>) in Korean). The <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard language</a> (<i>pyojuneo</i> or <i>pyojunmal</i>) of both South Korea and North Korea is based on the dialect of the area around Seoul, though the northern standard has been influenced by the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Pyongyang" title="Pyongyang">P'yŏngyang</a>. All dialects of Korean are similar to each other and at least partially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a>, though the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeju_dialect" title="Jeju dialect">dialect of Jeju Island</a> is divergent enough to be sometimes classified as a separate language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> One of the more salient differences between dialects is the use of tone: speakers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seoul_dialect" title="Seoul dialect">Seoul dialect</a> make use of vowel length, whereas speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongsang_dialect" title="Gyeongsang dialect">Gyeongsang dialect</a> maintain the <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> of Middle Korean. Some dialects are conservative, maintaining Middle Korean sounds (such as <i>z, β, ə</i>) which have been lost from the standard language, whereas others are highly innovative.</p>
<p>There is substantial evidence for a history of extensive <a href="/wiki/Dialect_levelling" title="Dialect levelling">dialect levelling</a>, or even <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> or intermixture of two or more originally distinct linguistic stocks, within the Korean language and its dialects. Many Korean dialects have basic vocabulary that is etymologically distinct from vocabulary of identical meaning in Standard Korean or other dialects, such as South Jeolla dialect <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kur/</span> vs. Standard Korean <span lang="ko"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%EC%9E%85" title="wikt:입">입</a></span> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ip/</span> "mouth" or Gyeongsang dialect <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t͡ɕʌŋ.ɡu.d͡ʑi/</span> vs. Standard Korean <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/puːt͡ɕʰu/</span> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garlic_chives" title="Garlic chives">garlic chives</a>". This suggests that the Korean Peninsula may have at one time been much more linguistically diverse than it is at present. See also the <a href="/wiki/Buyeo_languages" title="Buyeo languages">Buyeo languages</a> <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a>.</p>
<p>Nonetheless, the separation of the two Korean nations has resulted in increasing differences among the dialects that have emerged over time. Since the allies of the newly founded nations split the Korean peninsula in half after 1945, the newly formed Korean nations have since borrowed vocabulary extensively from their respective allies. As the Soviet Union helped industrialize North Korea and establish it as a communist state, the North Koreans would therefore borrow a number of Russian terms. Likewise, since the United States helped South Korea extensively to develop militarily, economically, and politically, South Koreans would therefore borrow extensively from English. The differences among northern and southern dialects have become so significant that many North Korean defectors reportedly have had great difficulty communicating with South Koreans after having initially settled into South Korea. In response to the diverging vocabularies, an app called Univoca was designed to help North Korean defectors learn South Korean terms by translating them into North Korean ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> More info can be found on the page <a href="/wiki/North%E2%80%93South_differences_in_the_Korean_language" title="North–South differences in the Korean language">North-South differences in the Korean language</a>.</p>
<p>Aside from the standard language, there are few clear boundaries between Korean dialects, and they are typically partially grouped according to the <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Korea" title="Regions of Korea">regions of Korea</a>.</p>

<p><sup>1</sup> The semivowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> are represented in Korean writing by modifications to vowel symbols (see below).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> symbol ⟨<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">◌͈</span>⟩ (a subscript double straight quotation mark, shown here with a placeholder circle) is used to denote the tensed consonants <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p͈/, /t͈/, /k͈/, /t͡ɕ͈/, /s͈/</span>. Its official use in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensions_to_the_IPA" title="Extensions to the IPA">Extensions to the IPA</a> is for <a href="/wiki/Fortis_and_lenis" title="Fortis and lenis">'strong'</a> articulation, but is used in the literature for <a href="/wiki/Faucalized_voice" title="Faucalized voice">faucalized voice</a>. The Korean consonants also have elements of <a href="/wiki/Stiff_voice" title="Stiff voice">stiff voice</a>, but it is not yet known how typical this is of faucalized consonants. They are produced with a partially constricted <a href="/wiki/Glottis" title="Glottis">glottis</a> and additional subglottal pressure in addition to tense vocal tract walls, laryngeal lowering, or other expansion of the larynx.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<th>Vowels preceded by intermediaries,<br/>
or diphthongs</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/je/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅖ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jɛ/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅒ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ja/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅑ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/wi/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅟ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/we/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅞ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/wɛ/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅙ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/wa/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅘ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɰi/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅢ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jo/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅛ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ju/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅠ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jə/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅕ</span>,  <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/wə/</span> <span lang="ko">ㅝ</span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_.E3.85.8F"><b><a href="#ref_.E3.85.8F">^*</a></b></span> <span lang="ko">ㅏ</span> is closer to a <a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">near-open central vowel</a> (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɐ]</span>), though ⟨a⟩ is still used for tradition.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allophones">Allophones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Allophones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> is aspirated <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[sʰ]</span> and becomes an <a href="/wiki/Alveolo-palatal_consonant" title="Alveolo-palatal consonant">alveolo-palatal</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɕʰ]</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[j]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i]</span> for most speakers (but see <a href="/wiki/North%E2%80%93South_differences_in_the_Korean_language" title="North–South differences in the Korean language">North–South differences in the Korean language</a>). This occurs with the tense fricative and all the affricates as well. At the end of a syllable, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> changes to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> (example: beoseot (<span lang="ko">버섯</span>) 'mushroom').</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/h/</span> may become a <a href="/wiki/Bilabial_consonant" title="Bilabial consonant">bilabial</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɸ]</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[o]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[u]</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Palatal_consonant" title="Palatal consonant">palatal</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ç]</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[j]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i]</span>, a <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[x]</span> before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɯ]</span>, a voiced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɦ]</span> between voiced sounds, and a <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[h]</span> elsewhere.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p, t, t͡ɕ, k/</span> become voiced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[b, d, d͡ʑ, ɡ]</span> between voiced sounds.</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m, n/</span> frequently denasalize to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[b, d]</span> at the beginnings of words.</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> becomes alveolar flap <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span> between vowels, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[l]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɭ]</span> at the end of a syllable or next to another <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span>. Note that a written syllable-final '<span lang="ko">ㄹ</span>', when followed by a vowel or a glide (<i>i.e.</i>, when the next character starts with '<span lang="ko">ㅇ</span>'), migrates to the next syllable and thus becomes <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span>.</p>
<p>Traditionally, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> was disallowed at the beginning of a word. It disappeared before <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[j]</span>, and otherwise became <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/n/</span>. However, the inflow of western <a href="/wiki/Konglish" title="Konglish">loanwords</a> changed the trend, and now word-initial <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> (mostly from English loanwords) are pronounced as a free variation of either <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[l]</span>. The traditional prohibition of word-initial <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> became a morphological rule called "initial law" (<span lang="ko">두음법칙</span>) in South Korea, which pertains to Sino-Korean vocabulary. Such words retain their word-initial <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> in North Korea.</p>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruents</a> (plosives, affricates, fricatives) at the end of a word are pronounced with <a href="/wiki/No_audible_release" title="No audible release">no audible release</a>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[p̚, t̚, k̚]</span>.</p>
<p>Plosive stops <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/p, t, k/</span> become nasal stops <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[m, n, ŋ]</span> before nasal stops.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a> spelling does not reflect these assimilatory pronunciation rules, but rather maintains the underlying, partly historical <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>. Given this, it is sometimes hard to tell which actual phonemes are present in a certain word.</p>
<p>One difference between the pronunciation standards of North and South Korea is the treatment of initial <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span>, and initial <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[n]</span>. For example,</p>
<ul>
<li>"labor" – north: <i><b>r</b>odong</i> (<span lang="ko">로동</span>), south: <i><b>n</b>odong</i> (<span lang="ko">노동</span>)</li>
<li>"history" – north: <i><b>ry</b>ŏksa</i> (<span lang="ko">력사</span>), south: <i><b>y</b>eoksa</i> (<span lang="ko">역사</span>)</li>
<li>"female" – north: <i><b>ny</b>ŏja</i> (<span lang="ko">녀자</span>), south: <i><b>y</b>eoja</i> (<span lang="ko">여자</span>)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphophonemics">Morphophonemics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Morphophonemics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morphophonology" title="Morphophonology">Morphophonology</a></div>
<p>Grammatical morphemes may change shape depending on the preceding sounds. Examples include <i>-eun/-neun</i> (<span lang="ko">-은/-는</span>) and <i>-i/-ga</i> (<span lang="ko">-이/-가</span>). Sometimes sounds may be inserted instead. Examples include <i>-eul/-reul</i> (<span lang="ko">-을/-를</span>), <i>-euro/-ro</i> (<span lang="ko">-으로/-로</span>), <i>-eseo/-seo</i> (<span lang="ko">-에서/-서</span>), <i>-ideunji/-deunji</i> (<span lang="ko">-이든지/-든지</span>) and <i>-iya/-ya</i> (<span lang="ko">-이야/-야</span>). However, <i>-euro/-ro</i> is somewhat irregular, since it will behave differently after a rieul consonant.</p>

<p>Some verbs may also change shape morphophonemically.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_grammar" title="Korean grammar">Korean grammar</a></div>
<p>Korean is an <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative language</a>. The Korean language is traditionally considered to have nine parts of speech. For details, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_parts_of_speech" title="Korean parts of speech">Korean parts of speech</a>. Modifiers generally precede the modified words, and in the case of verb modifiers, can be serially appended. The basic form of a Korean sentence is <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" title="Subject–object–verb">subject–object–verb</a>, but the verb is the only required and immovable element.</p>
<table>
<tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speech_levels_and_honorifics">Speech levels and honorifics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Speech levels and honorifics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_honorifics" title="Korean honorifics">Korean honorifics</a></div>
<p>The relationship between a speaker or writer and his or her subject and audience is paramount in Korean grammar. The relationship between speaker/writer and subject referent is reflected in <i><a href="/wiki/Honorific" title="Honorific">honorifics</a></i>, whereas that between speaker/writer and audience is reflected in <i>speech level</i>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Honorifics">Honorifics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Honorifics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When talking about someone superior in status, a speaker or writer usually uses special nouns or verb endings to indicate the subject's superiority. Generally, someone is superior in status if he/she is an older relative, a stranger of roughly equal or greater age, or an employer, teacher, customer, or the like. Someone is equal or inferior in status if he/she is a younger stranger, student, employee or the like. Nowadays, there are special endings which can be used on declarative, interrogative, and imperative sentences; and both honorific or normal sentences. They are made for easier and faster use of Korean.</p>
<p>Honorifics in traditional Korea were strictly hierarchical. The caste and estate systems possessed patterns and usages much more complex and stratified than those used today. The intricate structure of the Korean honorific system flourished in traditional culture and society. Honorifics in contemporary Korea are now used for people who are psychologically distant. Honorifics are also used for people who are superior in status. For example, older relatives, people who are older, teachers, and employers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_2006_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn_2006-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Speech_levels">Speech levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Speech levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are seven verb <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Korean_speech_levels" title="Korean speech levels"><i>speech levels</i> in Korean</a>, and each level has its own unique set of verb endings which are used to indicate the level of formality of a situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorifics" title="Honorifics">honorifics</a>—which are used to show respect towards the referent (the person spoken of) —<i>speech levels</i> are used to show respect towards a speaker's or writer's audience (the person spoken to). The names of the seven levels are derived from the non-honorific <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative</a> form of the verb <span lang="ko"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%ED%95%98%EB%8B%A4" title="wikt:하다">하다</a></span> (<i>hada</i>, "do") in each level, plus the suffix <span lang="ko">체</span> ("che", <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a>: <span lang="ko">體</span>), which means "style".</p>
<p>The highest six levels are generally grouped together as <i>jondaenmal</i> (<span lang="ko">존댓말</span>), whereas the lowest level (<i>haeche</i>, <span lang="ko">해체</span>) is called <i>banmal</i> (<span lang="ko">반말</span>) in Korean.</p>
<p>Nowadays, younger-generation speakers no longer feel obligated to lower their usual regard toward the referent. It is common to see younger people talk to their older relatives with banmal (<span lang="ko">반말</span>). This is not out of disrespect, but instead it shows the intimacy and the closeness of the relationship between the two speakers. Transformations in social structures and attitudes in today's rapidly changing society have brought about change in the way people speak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_2006_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn_2006-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociolinguistics">Sociolinguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sociolinguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_and_the_Korean_language">Gender and the Korean language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gender and the Korean language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In general, Korean lacks grammatical gender. As one of the few exceptions, third-person singular pronoun has two different forms: 그 <i>geu</i> (male) and 그녀 <i>geunyeo</i> (female). However, these terms were invented in the 20th century under the influence of foreign languages, and they seldom appear in colloquial speech.</p>
<p>However, one can still find stronger contrasts between the genders within Korean speech. Some examples of this can be seen in: (1) softer tone used by women in speech; (2) a married woman introducing herself as someone’s mother or wife, not with her own name; (3) the presence of gender differences in titles and occupational terms (for example, a <i>sajang</i> is a company president and <i>yŏsajang</i> is a female company president.); (4) females sometimes using more <a href="/wiki/Tag_question" title="Tag question">tag questions</a> and rising tones in statements, also seen in speech from children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Western societies, individuals tend to avoid expressions of power asymmetry, mutually addressing each other by their first names for the sake of solidarity. Between two people of asymmetrical status in a Korean society, people tend to emphasize differences in status for the sake of solidarity. Koreans prefer to use kinship terms rather than any other terms of reference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In traditional Korean society, women have long been in disadvantaged positions. Korean social structure traditionally was a patriarchically dominated family system that emphasized the maintenance of family lines. This structure has tended to separate roles of women from those of men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The core of the Korean vocabulary is made up of native Korean words. A significant proportion of the vocabulary, especially words that denote abstract ideas, are <a href="/wiki/Sino-Korean_vocabulary" title="Sino-Korean vocabulary">Sino-Korean words</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn-37">[37]</a></sup> either</p>
<ul>
<li>directly borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">written Chinese</a>, or</li>
<li>coined in Korea or Japan using <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>,</li>
</ul>
<p>The exact proportion of Sino-Korean vocabulary is a matter of debate. Sohn (2001) stated 50–60%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn-37">[37]</a></sup> Later, the same author (2006, p. 5) gives an even higher estimate of 65%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_2006_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn_2006-32">[32]</a></sup> Jeong Jae-do, one of the compilers of the dictionary <i>Urimal Keun Sajeon</i>, asserts that the proportion is not so high. He points out that Korean dictionaries compiled during the <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">colonial period</a> include many unused Sino-Korean words. In his estimation, the proportion of native Korean vocabulary in the Korean language might be as high as 70%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Korean has two <a href="/wiki/Korean_numerals" title="Korean numerals">numeral systems</a>: one native, and one borrowed from Sino-Korean.</p>
<p>To a much lesser extent, some words have also been borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a> and other languages. Conversely, the Korean language itself has also contributed some loanwords to other languages, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Tsushima_dialect" title="Tsushima dialect">Tsushima dialect</a> of Japanese.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The vast majority of loanwords other than Sino-Korean come from modern times, approximately 90% of which are from <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn-37">[37]</a></sup> Many words have also been borrowed from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_languages" title="European languages">Western languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> via <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> (<span lang="ko">아르바이트</span> (<i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AB%E3%83%90%E3%82%A4%E3%83%88" title="wikt:アルバイト">areubaiteu</a></i>) "part-time job", <span lang="ko">알레르기</span> (<i>allereugi</i>) "allergy", <span lang="ko">기브스</span> (<i>gibseu</i> or <i>gibuseu</i>) "plaster cast used for broken bones"). Some Western words were borrowed indirectly via Japanese during the <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Japanese occupation of Korea</a>, taking a Japanese sound pattern, for example "dozen" &gt; <span lang="ja-Kana">ダース</span> <i>dāsu</i> &gt; <span lang="ko">다스</span> <i>daseu</i>. Most indirect Western borrowings are now written according to current "Hangulization" rules for the respective Western language, as if borrowed directly. There are a few more complicated borrowings such as "German(y)" (see <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Germany#Korean" title="Names of Germany">names of Germany</a>), the first part of whose <a href="/wiki/Exonym_and_endonym" title="Exonym and endonym">endonym</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈd̥ɔɪ̯t͡ʃʷ.la̠ntʰ]</span> the Japanese approximated using the <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> <span lang="ja">獨逸</span> <i>doitsu</i> that were then accepted into the Korean language by their Sino-Korean pronunciation: <span lang="ko"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8D%A8" title="wikt:獨">獨</a></span> <i>dok</i> + <span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%80%B8" title="wikt:逸">逸</a></span> <i>il</i> = <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%EB%8F%85%EC%9D%BC" title="wikt:독일">Dogil</a></i>. In South Korean official use, a number of other Sino-Korean country names have been replaced with phonetically oriented "Hangeulizations" of the countries' endonyms or English names.</p>
<p>Because of such a prevalence of English in modern Korean culture and society, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexical_borrowing" title="Lexical borrowing">lexical borrowing</a> is inevitable. English-derived Korean, or '<a href="/wiki/Konglish" title="Konglish">Konglish</a>' (<span lang="ko">콩글리쉬</span>), is increasingly used. The vocabulary of the Korean language is roughly 5% loanwords (excluding Sino-Korean vocabulary).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohn_2006_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sohn_2006-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Korean uses words adapted from English in ways that may seem strange to native English speakers. For example, in soccer <i>heading</i> (<span lang="ko">헤딩</span>) is used as a noun meaning a 'header', whereas <i>fighting</i> (<span lang="ko">화이팅 / 파이팅</span>) is a term of encouragement like 'come on'/'go (on)' in English. Something that is 'service' (<span lang="ko">서비스</span>) is free or 'on the house'. A building referred to as an 'apart-uh' (<span lang="ko">아파트</span>) is an 'apartment' (but in fact refers to a residence more akin to a condominium) and a type of pencil that is called a 'sharp' (<span lang="ko">샤프</span>) is a mechanical pencil.</p>
<p>North Korean vocabulary shows a tendency to prefer native Korean over Sino-Korean or foreign borrowings, especially with recent political objectives aimed at eliminating foreign influences on the Korean language in the North. In the early years, the North Korean government tried to eliminate Sino-Korean words. Consequently, South Korean may have several Sino-Korean or foreign borrowings which are not in North Korean.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_Braille" title="Korean Braille">Korean Braille</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hangul_consonant_and_vowel_tables" title="Hangul consonant and vowel tables">Hangul consonant and vowel tables</a></div>
<p>Before the creation of <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>, people in Korea (known as <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> at the time) primarily wrote using <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> alongside native phonetic writing systems that predate Hangul by hundreds of years, including <a href="/wiki/Idu_script" title="Idu script">idu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyangchal" title="Hyangchal">hyangchal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gugyeol" title="Gugyeol">gugyeol</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gakpil&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gakpil (page does not exist)">gakpil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phonetic_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-phonetic-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> However, due to the fundamental differences between the Korean and Chinese languages, and the large number of characters needed to be learned, there was much difficulty in learning how to write using Chinese characters for the lower classes, who often didn't have the privilege of education. To assuage this problem, <a href="/wiki/Sejong_the_Great" title="Sejong the Great">King Sejong</a> created the unique alphabet known as Hangul to promote literacy among the common people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hangul was denounced and looked down upon by the <i><a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">yangban</a></i> aristocracy who deemed it too easy to learn,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> but it gained widespread use among the common class,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and was widely used to print popular novels which were enjoyed by the common class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> With growing Korean nationalism in the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Gabo_Reform" title="Gabo Reform">Gabo Reformists</a>' push, and the promotion of Hangul in schools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> in 1894, Hangul displaced <i>hanja</i> as Korea's national script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <i>Hanja</i> is still used to a certain extent in South Korea where it is sometimes combined with Hangul, but this method is slowly declining in use despite students learning <i>hanja</i> in school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Below is a chart of the Korean alphabet's symbols and their canonical <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> values:</p>

<p>Modern Korean is written with spaces between <a href="/wiki/Word" title="Word">words</a>, a feature not found in Chinese or Japanese. <a href="/wiki/Korean_punctuation" title="Korean punctuation">Korean punctuation</a> marks are almost identical to Western ones. Traditionally, Korean was written in columns, from top to bottom, right to left, but is now usually written in rows, from left to right, top to bottom.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_between_North_Korean_and_South_Korean">Differences between North Korean and South Korean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Differences between North Korean and South Korean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/North%E2%80%93South_differences_in_the_Korean_language" title="North–South differences in the Korean language">North–South differences in the Korean language</a></div>
<p>The Korean language used in the North and the South exhibits differences in pronunciation, spelling, grammar and vocabulary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronunciation">Pronunciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pronunciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In North Korea, <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalization</a> of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/si/</span> is optional, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t͡ɕ/</span> can be pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[z]</span> between vowels.</p>
<p>Words that are written the same way may be pronounced differently, such as the examples below. The pronunciations below are given in <a href="/wiki/Revised_Romanization_of_Korean" title="Revised Romanization of Korean">Revised Romanization</a>, <a href="/wiki/McCune%E2%80%93Reischauer" title="McCune–Reischauer">McCune–Reischauer</a> and Hangul, the last of which represents what the Hangul would be if one were to write the word as pronounced.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">Word</th>
<th rowspan="2">Meaning</th>
<th colspan="4">Pronunciation</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>North (RR/MR)</th>
<th>North (Hangul)</th>
<th>South (RR/MR)</th>
<th>South (Hangul)</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko">읽고</span></td>
<td>to read<br/>
(continuative form)</td>
<td>il<b>k</b>o (il<b>k</b>o)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">일코</span></td>
<td>il<b>kk</b>o (il<b>kk</b>o)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">일꼬</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko">압록강</span></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Yalu_River" title="Yalu River">Amnok River</a></td>
<td>am<b>r</b>okgang (am<b>r</b>okkang)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">암록깡</span></td>
<td>am<b>n</b>okkang (am<b>n</b>okkang)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">암녹깡</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko">독립</span></td>
<td>independence</td>
<td>dong<b>r</b>ip (tong<b>r</b>ip)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">동립</span></td>
<td>dong<b>n</b>ip (tong<b>n</b>ip)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">동닙</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko">관념</span></td>
<td>idea / sense / conception</td>
<td>gwa<b>ll</b>yeom (kwa<b>ll</b>yŏm)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">괄렴</span></td>
<td>gwa<b>nn</b>yeom (kwa<b>nn</b>yŏm)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">관념</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko">혁신적*</span></td>
<td>innovative</td>
<td>hyeoksin<b>jj</b>eok (hyŏksin<b>tch</b>ŏk)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">혁씬쩍</span></td>
<td>hyeoksin<b>j</b>eok (hyŏksin<b>j</b>ŏk)</td>
<td><span lang="ko">혁씬적</span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>* Similar pronunciation is used in the North whenever the hanja "<span lang="ko">的</span>" is attached to a Sino-Korean word ending in <span lang="ko">ㄴ</span>, <span lang="ko">ㅁ</span> or <span lang="ko">ㅇ</span>. (In the South, this rule only applies when it is attached to any single-character Sino-Korean word.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling">Spelling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Spelling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some words are spelled differently by the North and the South, but the pronunciations are the same.</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th colspan="2">Word</th>
<th rowspan="2">Meaning</th>
<th rowspan="2">Pronunciation (RR/MR)</th>
<th rowspan="2">Remarks</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>North spelling</th>
<th>South spelling</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">해빛</span></td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">햇빛</span></td>
<td>sunshine</td>
<td>haeppit (haepit)</td>
<td>The "sai siot" ('<span lang="ko">ㅅ</span>' used for indicating sound change) is almost never written out in the North.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">벗꽃</span></td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">벚꽃</span></td>
<td>cherry blossom</td>
<td>beotkkot (pŏtkkot)</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">못읽다</span></td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">못 읽다</span></td>
<td>cannot read</td>
<td>modikda (modikta)</td>
<td>Spacing.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">한나산</span></td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">한라산</span></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Hallasan" title="Hallasan">Hallasan</a></td>
<td>hallasan (hallasan)</td>
<td>When a <span lang="ko">ㄴㄴ</span> combination is pronounced as <i>ll</i>, the original Hangul spelling is kept in the North, whereas the Hangul is changed in the South.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">규률</span></td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">규율</span></td>
<td>rules</td>
<td>gyuyul (kyuyul)</td>
<td>In words where the original hanja is spelt "<span lang="ko">렬</span>" or "<span lang="ko">률</span>" and follows a vowel, the initial <span lang="ko">ㄹ</span> is not pronounced in the North, making the pronunciation identical with that in the South where the <span lang="ko">ㄹ</span> is dropped in the spelling.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_and_pronunciation">Spelling and pronunciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Spelling and pronunciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some words have different spellings and pronunciations in the North and the South, some of which were given in the "Phonology" section above:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th colspan="4">Word</th>
<th rowspan="2">Meaning</th>
<th rowspan="2">Remarks</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>North spelling</th>
<th>North pronun.</th>
<th>South spelling</th>
<th>South pronun.</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">력량</span></td>
<td>ryeongryang (ryŏngryang)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">역량</span></td>
<td>yeongnyang (yŏngnyang)</td>
<td>strength</td>
<td>Initial <i>r'</i>s are dropped if followed by <i>i</i> or <i>y</i> in the South Korean version of Korean.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">로동</span></td>
<td>rodong (rodong)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">노동</span></td>
<td>nodong (nodong)</td>
<td>work</td>
<td>Initial <i>r'</i>s are demoted to an <i>n</i> if not followed by <i>i</i> or <i>y</i> in the South Korean version of Korean.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">원쑤</span></td>
<td>wonssu (wŏnssu)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">원수</span></td>
<td>wonsu (wŏnsu)</td>
<td>mortal enemy</td>
<td>"Mortal enemy" and "<a href="/wiki/Wonsu" title="Wonsu">field marshal</a>" are homophones in the South. Possibly to avoid referring to <a href="/wiki/Kim_Il-sung" title="Kim Il-sung">Kim Il-sung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong-il" title="Kim Jong-il">Kim Jong-il</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong-un" title="Kim Jong-un">Kim Jong-un</a> as the enemy, the second syllable of "enemy" is written and pronounced <span lang="ko">쑤</span> in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">라지오</span></td>
<td>rajio (rajio)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">라디오</span></td>
<td>radio (radio)</td>
<td>radio</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">우</span></td>
<td>u (u)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">위</span></td>
<td>wi (wi)</td>
<td>on; above</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">안해</span></td>
<td>anhae (anhae)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">아내</span></td>
<td>anae (anae)</td>
<td>wife</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">꾸바</span></td>
<td>kkuba (kkuba)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">쿠바</span></td>
<td>kuba (k'uba)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a></td>
<td>When transcribing foreign words from languages that do not have contrasts between aspirated and unaspirated stops, North Koreans generally use tensed stops for the unaspirated ones while South Koreans use aspirated stops in both cases.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">페</span></td>
<td>pe (p'e)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">폐</span></td>
<td>pye (p'ye), pe (p'e)</td>
<td>lungs</td>
<td>In the case where <i>ye</i> comes after a consonant, such as in <i>hye</i> and <i>pye</i>, it is pronounced without the palatal approximate. North Korean orthography reflect this pronunciation nuance.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In general, when transcribing place names, North Korea tends to use the pronunciation in the original language more than South Korea, which often uses the pronunciation in English. For example:</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar_2">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some grammatical constructions are also different:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th colspan="4">Word</th>
<th rowspan="2">Meaning</th>
<th rowspan="2">Remarks</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>North spelling</th>
<th>North pronun.</th>
<th>South spelling</th>
<th>South pronun.</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">되였다</span></td>
<td>doeyeotda (toeyŏtta)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">되었다</span></td>
<td>doeeotda (toeŏtta)</td>
<td>past tense of <span lang="ko">되다</span> (doeda/toeda), "to become"</td>
<td>All similar grammar forms of verbs or adjectives that end in <span lang="ko">ㅣ</span> in the stem (i.e. <span lang="ko">ㅣ</span>, <span lang="ko">ㅐ</span>, <span lang="ko">ㅔ</span>, <span lang="ko">ㅚ</span>, <span lang="ko">ㅟ</span> and <span lang="ko">ㅢ</span>) in the North use <span lang="ko">여</span> instead of the South's <span lang="ko">어</span>.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">고마와요</span></td>
<td>gomawayo (komawayo)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">고마워요</span></td>
<td>gomawoyo (komawŏyo)</td>
<td>thanks</td>
<td><span lang="ko">ㅂ</span>-irregular verbs in the North use <span lang="ko">와</span> (wa) for all those with a positive ending vowel; this only happens in the South if the verb stem has only one syllable.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">할가요</span></td>
<td>halgayo (halkayo)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">할까요</span></td>
<td>halkkayo (halkkayo)</td>
<td>Shall we do?</td>
<td>Although the Hangul differ, the pronunciations are the same (i.e. with the tensed <span lang="ko">ㄲ</span> sound).</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary_2">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some vocabulary is different between the North and the South:</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th colspan="4">Word</th>
<th rowspan="2">Meaning</th>
<th rowspan="2">Remarks</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>North word</th>
<th>North pronun.</th>
<th>South word</th>
<th>South pronun.</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">문화주택</span></td>
<td>munhwajutaek (munhwajut'aek)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">아파트</span></td>
<td>apateu (ap'at'ŭ)</td>
<td>Apartment</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">아빠트</span> (appateu/appat'ŭ) is also used in the North.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">조선말</span></td>
<td>joseonmal (josŏnmal)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">한국말</span></td>
<td>han-gukmal (han'gukmal)</td>
<td>Korean language</td>
<td>한국말 is also used in the South</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">곽밥</span></td>
<td>gwakbap (kwakpap)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">도시락</span></td>
<td>dosirak (tosirak)</td>
<td>lunch box</td>
<td></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KP">동무</span></td>
<td>dongmu (tongmu)</td>
<td><span lang="ko-Hang-KR">친구</span></td>
<td>chin-gu (ch'in-gu)</td>
<td>Friend</td>
<td><span lang="ko">동무</span> was originally a non-ideological word for "friend" used all over the Korean peninsula, but North Koreans later adopted it as the equivalent of the Communist term of address "<a href="/wiki/Comrade" title="Comrade">comrade</a>". As a result, to South Koreans today the word has a heavy political tinge, and so they have shifted to using other words for friend like <i>chingu</i> (<span lang="ko">친구</span>) or <i>beot</i> (<span lang="ko">벗</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Punctuation">Punctuation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Punctuation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the North, <a href="/wiki/Guillemet" title="Guillemet">guillemets</a> <span lang="ko-KP">《</span> and <span lang="ko-KP">》</span> are the symbols used for quotes; in the South, quotation marks equivalent to the English ones, <span lang="ko-KR">“</span> and <span lang="ko-KR">”</span>, are standard, although <span lang="ko-KR">『 』</span> and <span lang="ko-KR">「 」</span> are also used.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Study_by_non-native_learners">Study by non-native learners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korean_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Study by non-native learners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For native English speakers, Korean is generally considered to be one of the most difficult languages to master despite the relative ease of learning Hangul. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>' <a href="/wiki/Defense_Language_Institute" title="Defense Language Institute">Defense Language Institute</a> places Korean in Category IV, which also includes <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>. This means that 63 weeks of instruction (as compared to just 25 weeks for <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>) are required to bring an English-speaking student to a limited working level of proficiency in which he or she has "sufficient capability to meet routine social demands and limited job requirements" and "can deal with concrete topics in past, present, and future tense."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Service_Institute" title="Foreign Service Institute">Foreign Service Institute</a>'s School of Language Studies places Korean in Category IV, the highest level of difficulty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The study of the Korean language in the United States is dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_American" title="Korean American">Korean American</a> <a href="/wiki/Heritage_language_learning" title="Heritage language learning">heritage language students</a>; in 2007 they were estimated to form over 80% of all students of the language at non-military universities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Sejong_Institute" title="Sejong Institute">Sejong Institutes</a> in the United States have noted a sharp rise in the number of people of other ethnic backgrounds studying Korean between 2009 and 2011; they attribute this to <a href="/wiki/Korean_Wave" title="Korean Wave">rising popularity of South Korean music and television shows</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two widely used tests of Korean as a foreign language: the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Language_Proficiency_Test" title="Korean Language Proficiency Test">Korean Language Proficiency Test</a> (KLPT) and the <a href="/wiki/Test_of_Proficiency_in_Korean" title="Test of Proficiency in Korean">Test of Proficiency in Korean</a> (TOPIK). The Korean Language Proficiency Test, an examination aimed at assessing non-native speakers' competence in Korean, was instituted in 1997; 17,000 people applied for the 2005 sitting of the examination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The TOPIK was first administered in 1997 and was taken by 2,274 people. Since then the total number of people who have taken the TOPIK has surpassed 1 million, with more than 150,000 candidates taking the test in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
